Huxter Family &amp; Descendants Tree
Huxter Family &amp; Descendants Tree
Nfld----Canada
The Family tree starts in Kent, England
and stops at Springdale, Nfld with
Branches across Canada.
Map Of Newfoundland
The Huxter’s came from Luxembourg to Kent
England sometime before 1580. Kent and
Essex is north west of London. The family
later moved to Bridport, south east coast
of England.
Huxter-a surname of England, from middle
English-to bargain hence a seller of small
goods. Peddler—Hawker.
Bridport stands where the river Brit and
Asker meet at the westerly end of Dorset.
The region’s rich soil and temperate
climate proved ideal for cultivation and
production of flax and hemp, two crops
which had a profound influence on
Bridport’s prosperity.
Bridport Harbour prospered from its
fishing and maritime trade. Some Bridport
merchants deliberately competed against
colonial suppliers to Newfoundland by
assembling cargoes of goods to Virginia,
USA. Bridport traded with Newfoundland and
Virginia from the 16<sup>th</sup> century
to the mid-19t<sup>h</sup> century.
Bridport ship carried fishnets, twine and
rope to Newfoundland and Virginia,
returned with fish, timber and tobacco.
Records show that a salt house existed at
Bridport to store salt for Newfoundland
cod fisheries. Supplies of line, rope and
sailcloth were taken from Bridport harbor
to the Grand Banks, salt forming part of
the ballast. Salt cod was then brought
back as a valuable part of the winter diet
A coastal boat was built in 1948 and named
the SS. Springdale, after the Community.
For many people the S.S. Springdale was
considered a lifeline for the remote
communities it served and was a welcome
sight following the harsh winter and ice
conditions experienced each year. During
its years in service, the SS Springdale
was described as a workhorse that
performed honorable service to coastal
residents.
S.S. Springdale
The Compiler of these pages did not
expect, when he commenced tracing the line
of his ancestry, running back in some
respects beyond the first immigrant of the
name, to bring anything before the public.
After years of research, so much
interesting material was discovered that I
decided to continue. There turned out to
be many more Family Members than
originally though.
We should honor the memory of those who,
gave us our being and their order of birth
in the family is denoted by Roman
numerals. Each person is numbered in
common Figures, and the order of birth in
the family by Roman Letters. This
arrangement will enable one to know the
branch which he or she is connected, and
by referring back to the preceding
generation in his or her branch, they will
follow back the line to their ancestry.

26, 2006, Toronto
Nathaniel Huxter Samuel Huxter
Samuel was born in a fishing community on
the southern shore of Fogo Island, Nfld in
a place called Seldom-Come-By in 1872.
Sarah was born at Grand Bank in Fortune
Bay, on the Southern Coast of Nfld in
1886. Samuel (1) married Irene Penny from
Springdale In 1899 and they had one son,
Stephen born in 1900. Stephen lived at
Badger, Nfld. Irene died in 1901. Samuel
(2) married Sarah Hickman Hiscock.
Sarah received her teaching Certificate
when she was only 15 years old in 1901..
Samuel and Sarah were married in George
Brown’s house in Springdale in 1903, Sarah
was only 17 years old. Samuel and Sarah
had 11 children. All but one child was
born at Springdale. In 1904 there was a
Forest fire that burned Springdale to the
ground. Sarah was pregnant in the summer
of 1904 and she went back to Grand Bank to
have her first child. Marion was born on
Dec 3, 1904 at Grand Bank
In 1908 Sarah helped to organize the
ladies aid and became secretary-treasurer.
She was also very active within the United
Church. Samuel and his brother Nathaniel
worked in Sawmills in Hall’s Bay in 1898
In the mid 1920’s Samuel hurt his back and
could not work in the woods or any heavy
lifting, Nathaniel was the Warden at
Indian Falls and he gave up his job so his
brother could have it.
In 1928 grandfather became the warden and
for the next 22 years he was regarded as
one of Nfld’s most efficient wardens. He
was an authority on wildlife and he took
his job with great seriousness, impressing
at all times upon sportsmen, the necessity
of not only preserving game, but the
forest as well. He was particularly
careful about fire in the woods, and had a
special fire place and galley for the use
of all who came to the Falls. In his long
period of service as game warden he won
many friends. Fishman who went to the
Falls not only brought away salmon with
them, but many happy memories of the
pleasant hours spent in his cheerful
company. And should a fishman fail to get
a salmon, as is all too often the case, he
received a measure of philosophic comfort
that helped make up for his
disappointment.
The population of Springdale in 1908 was
373 made up of 24 Church of England, 341
Methodists, 3 Salvation Army and 1
Presbyterian, 1 pauper, 1 deaf mute, 1
clergyman and one teacher. The town had 63
dwelling for 65 families, 129 barns and
outhouse, 130, acres of improved land on
which was produced 68 tons of hay, 604
barrels of spuds and 120 barrels of
turnips. The town also had 76 dogs
employed in hauling wood, 17 horses, 35
cows, 10 bulls, 164 sheep, 36 pigs, 45
goats and 600 poultry. There were 3
sawmills employing 47 persons and the
average income was $50.00 per head or
$275.00 per family per year. $1.00 paid
the light bill for the year (4 gal. of
kerosene ) and the only thing the people
wore on their feet was skin that came off
the cow they killed the year before—and
the wool that came off the sheep turned
into knitted socks and stockings Life was
Hard
Samuel And Sarah had a big house with a
large kitchen and a big living room, one
bed room down stairs and five bedrooms
upstairs, three on the bay side and two on
the other side. My grandmother kept goats,
sheep, hens and ducks. In the twenties and
thirties the land was used for vegetable
and hay.
